## PART A — Foundations & Core Coaching (Articles 1–25)

## 1. Concurrent Training & Interference Management

**PRINCIPLE:** Concurrent training success is a problem of stress management, recovery windows, and energy allocation — not a choice between strength and endurance. (Art. 1)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Concurrent training: deliberate pairing of resistance training + cardiorespiratory development within the same week
- Interference: localized muscular fatigue or CNS overlap that degrades strength output or technical quality
- Stress consolidation: grouping highest-stress sessions into same 24-hour windows to protect recovery days

**Rules:**
- IF sport/cardio volume < 3 hrs/week → interference risk LOW; full lifting program viable.
- IF sport/cardio volume 3–6 hrs/week → interference risk MODERATE; begin reducing accessory work.
- IF sport/cardio volume > 6 hrs/week → interference risk HIGH; cap compound sets at 2–4 per Tier 1 lift; remove redundant accessory work first.
- IF sessions same-day → separate hard lifting from hard conditioning by ≥ 4–6 hrs (default 6 hrs); lift FIRST if strength retention is the priority goal.
- IF gap between sessions < 2 hrs → HIGH risk; move to separate days.
- IF fatigue is high OR bar speed clearly reduced OR strength dropped multiple sessions → separate hard lifting and conditioning onto different days.
- IF conditioning is block priority → conditioning can come first; reduce lifting volume that session.
- IF high-impact conditioning (pavement running, chaotic sparring, hard COD) → HIGH interference; cap or substitute with low-eccentric option.
- IF low-eccentric modality (cycling, sled, swimming, controlled rowing) → LOW-VERY LOW interference; compatible with heavy lifting blocks.
- IF caloric deficit + multi-quality training → HIGH risk; increase carbohydrate around double-session days; fuel between sessions.
- IF strength losses on primary lifts ≥ 3 consecutive weeks → trigger coach review.
- IF morning readiness low ≥ 2 weeks + rising resting HR → trigger coach review.
- IF recovery markers decline → reduce total working sets 15–30%; shift conditioning to lower-impact modalities; consolidate stress into fewer days.

**Tables / landmarks:**

| Interference Factor | Low Risk | Moderate Risk | High Risk |
|---|---|---|---|
| Weekly sport/cardio volume | < 3 hrs | 3–6 hrs | > 6 hrs |
| Conditioning modality | Cycling, sled, swim, row | Elliptical, turf circuits | Pavement run, sparring, COD |
| Session gap (same day) | > 8 hrs or separate days | 4–6 hrs | < 2 hrs |
| Lifting volume | Low vol, high intensity, RIR 2–3 | Moderate vol + some accessories | High vol compounds, ≥ 5 hard sets, or failure |
| Caloric balance | Maintenance or surplus | Small controlled deficit | Aggressive deficit |

**Conditioning modality recovery costs:**
- Running: HIGH joint stress, HIGH interference; best for runners; moderate BJJ transfer
- Cycling: LOW interference, LOW joint stress; strong Zone 2 tool
- Rowing: MODERATE interference (back + grip); HIGH BJJ transfer
- Swimming: VERY LOW interference; moderate BJJ transfer (breath control)
- Sled: VERY LOW interference, LOW joint stress; HIGH BJJ transfer (leg drive)
- Circuits: HIGH interference if poorly designed; HIGH BJJ transfer when positional-drill-based

**Volume caps during high sport stress:**
- Tier 1 compound sets: 2–4 working sets; RIR 1–3; load ~80–90% 1RM
- High-intensity conditioning: limit to 1–2 sessions/week; use Zone 2 for additional aerobic work
- Hard intervals: 4–6 rounds starting range (adjust for readiness and schedule)
- BJJ open mat: reduce weight-room volume 24–48 hrs prior if hard rounds expected
- Competition camp: shift lifting to maintenance (lower total volume, preserve intensity, avoid soreness)

**Goal-agnostic note:** Applies to any athlete combining two or more training qualities. "Sport" can be BJJ, running, cycling, obstacle racing, skill work, or any high-stress secondary modality. Examples using BJJ are illustrations only.

---

## 2. Movement Screening & Pain-Based Modification

**PRINCIPLE:** Movement quality is the non-negotiable entry gate to load progression; non-diagnostic screening routes the athlete to safer patterns without creating diagnoses or medical claims. (Art. 2)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Movement gate: observable quality filter assessing whether a pattern is functional, dysfunctional, or painful before external load is added
- Pain Gate Protocol: decision tree triggered when any symptom arises during a movement screen or working set
- Non-diagnostic: the framework identifies compensation and routing; it does NOT name pathology

**Rules — Pattern Gates:**

| Pattern | Pass Criteria | Common Alteration Signal | Programming Response |
|---|---|---|---|
| Squat | Heels flat, upright torso, hips below parallel smooth | Heels lift, forward lean, lumbar round | Front squat or SSB box squat |
| Hip Hinge | Flat spine, hips drive back, shoulders packed | Lumbar round, knees forward early, bar drifts | Block pulls or neutral-grip hex-bar DL |
| Horizontal/Overhead Press | Lockout without back arch, smooth push | Shoulder shrug, lumbar arch, elbow flare | Neutral-grip DB floor press or landmine |
| Pull | Full scapular retraction, chest meets bar, shoulders down | Shoulders roll forward, head juts, grip fails | Neutral-grip pull-ups or straps |
| Rotational/Core | Smooth rotation with square pelvis, stable brace | Lumbar hyperextension, excessive hip twist | Static hollow body or loaded carries |

**Rules — Pain Gate Protocol:**
- IF sharp, radiating, locking, giving-way, numbness, or tingling → STOP affected pattern immediately; do NOT test with reduced load; pause loading that pattern family; route to coach/clinician BEFORE any progression.
- IF mild, dull ache or general joint awareness (no locking, no sharp sensation, no radiating) → route to supervised modification; test grip/stance adjustment first; if more tolerable, may proceed with caution; if discomfort continues → change loading vector (different variation) or shorten ROM (floor blocks/box targets).

**Volume/intensity rules for modified patterns:**
- Sets: 2–3 working sets per modified exercise (minimize cumulative fatigue on recovering structures)
- Intensity: starting range 70–80% of the alternative lift's 1RM
- RIR: enforce RIR 2–4 (stay well away from absolute failure)
- Frequency: limit loaded exposures for sensitive pattern family to 1–2 sessions/week

**Sport-specific screening triggers:**
- Guard-player lower-back stiffness → swap conventional DL for elevated block pulls or KB hinges
- Shoulder irritation from framing → swap flat barbell bench for landmine or push-up handles; screen for smooth scapular retraction
- Knee sensitivity from takedowns/twisting → drop bilateral barbell squat depth; use vertical-shin variants (DB box squat, step-up)

**Coach/clinician review triggers (immediate):**
- Radiating symptoms (numbness, tingling, shooting) during or after loading any pattern
- Acute joint instability (locking, painful clicking, buckling)
- Persistent pain continuously outside training hours during daily tasks
- No variation within a pattern family can be executed tolerably

**Goal-agnostic note:** Applies to any athlete, any goal. Examples use BJJ/grappling as illustrations. The pain-gate and substitution logic applies equally to a marathon runner, powerlifter, or calisthenics athlete.

---

## 3. Exercise Substitution

**PRINCIPLE:** Exercise substitution preserves the training stimulus by matching primary joint actions and movement patterns using a lower-stress variation — no single lift is mandatory. (Art. 3)

**Definitions (internal):**
- 7 Movement pattern families: Squat, Hinge, Press, Pull, Carry, Core, Conditioning
- Substitution hierarchy: pattern match → goal preservation → loading vector adjustment → handle/grip freedom → ROM constraints → equipment cross-match

**Rules — Substitution Decision Tree:**
1. Match pattern archetype (squat stays squat, hinge stays hinge).
2. Preserve training goal (heavy strength slot stays heavy strength, not light balance drill).
3. Adjust loading vector if joint irritation (change bar position, move from bilateral to unilateral, floor to blocks).
4. Use independent handles (DBs, KBs, rings) if fixed bar causes wrist/elbow/shoulder awareness.
5. Implement ROM constraints (boxes, pins, floor contact) to train within tolerable range.
6. Match available equipment (never prescribe specialty bar unavailable in athlete's facility).
7. Keep progression trackable across weeks (substitute should allow repeatable loads and clear rep targets).

**Substitution routing table:**

| Constraint | Primary Lift | Replacement Options | Coaching Note |
|---|---|---|---|
| Lower-back irritation (squat) | Barbell Back Squat | Front Squat, SSB Box Squat | Upright torso reduces lumbar demand |
| Knee irritation (squat) | Barbell Front Squat | Box Squat, Reverse Lunge | Vertical shin reduces forward knee travel |
| Lower-back irritation (hinge) | Conventional DL | Elevated Hex-Bar DL, Block Pull | Elevated start reduces setup depth |
| High sport fatigue (hinge) | Barbell RDL | 45° Back Extension, Cable Pull-Through | Preserves posterior chain at lower systemic cost |
| Shoulder irritation (press) | Barbell Bench | Neutral-Grip DB Floor Press, Push-ups on Handles | Floor blocks extreme ROM; independent handles allow rotation |
| Lower-back or grip fatigue (pull) | Pendlay Row, Heavy DB Row | Chest-Supported DB Row, Strapped Row, Machine Pullover | Supports torso or bypasses grip as limiter |
| Impact sensitivity (conditioning) | Road Running | Cycling, Swimming, Rowing, Sled | Lower impact; preserves conditioning work |
| Guard-player hinge fatigue (BJJ) | Conventional DL | Elevated High-Handle Hex-Bar DL, Heavy KB Swing | Reduces deep trunk setup demand |
| Underhook/framing shoulder irritation | Barbell Bench, OHP | Neutral-Grip DB Floor Press, Landmine Press | Tolerable pressing path |
| Gi-grip fatigue before sparring | Heavy Row (double overhand) | Strapped Row, Zercher Carry | Preserves upper back without grip failure |

**Coach/clinician review triggers:**
- Every substitution within a pattern family triggers joint irritation or pain
- Neurological symptoms (numbness, tingling, shooting) across multiple modifications
- Pain or swelling persists > 48 hrs after using low-stress variations
- Sudden severe ROM loss or structural locking during unloaded movement

**Goal-agnostic note:** Examples cite BJJ/grappling contexts. Logic applies to any constraint: runner with knee irritation, powerlifter with shoulder impingement, endurance athlete with lower-back stiffness.

---

## 4. Barbell Program Design — Exercise Tiers, Loading, & Volume

**PRINCIPLE:** Systematic tier-based organization of volume, intensity, and frequency controls training stress and ensures high-quality adaptation without overtraining. (Art. 4)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Tier 1: Primary compound heavy lifts (highest neural + systemic cost)
- Tier 2: Supplemental/structural (pattern support, unilateral, lower systemic cost)
- Tier 3: Isolation and active recovery (low systemic cost, high rep, tissue health)
- Training Max (TM): conservative 1RM anchor = 90–95% of verified/estimated 1RM

**Rules — Tier Hierarchy:**

| Tier | Examples | Sets | Reps | RIR | Priority |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Tier 1 | Back Squat, Trap-Bar DL, Bench, OHP | 2–5 | 3–6 | 1–3 | Executed FIRST; never failure except planned |
| Tier 2 | BSS, DB Floor Press, RDL, Split Squat | 2–4 | 6–10 | 1–4 | After Tier 1; pattern support, balance |
| Tier 3 | Calf raise, curl, face pull | varies | 10–20 | 0–4 | Lowest cost; avoid failure if fatigue or joint irritation high |

**Rules — Loading:**
- IF Tier 1 → load 80–90% TM as starting range; adjust by sport load, recovery, training age.
- IF TM = 90–95% verified/estimated 1RM → working sets land ~80–85% true 1RM (sweet spot for motor unit recruitment without excessive fatigue).
- IF advanced athlete + sport stress high → reduce accessory volume BEFORE reducing Tier 1 intensity.
- IF movement quality declines or pain → stop; apply movement screening rules; substitute before recalculating load.

**Rules — Double Progression (primarily Tier 2):**
- Assign rep range (e.g., 4–6 reps). Athlete builds reps within range at target RIR.
- IF athlete hits TOP of rep range for ALL sets with clean technique + target RIR → increase load by 2–5%.
- IF load increases → reps drop back to bottom of range; rebuild upward.
- Rep ranges should be tight: 4–6, 6–8, 8–12 (NOT 5–12 — physiological stimulus too different across wide range).

**Rules — Step Loading (primarily Tier 1):**
- Hold BOTH load AND reps static for 2–4 weeks (one microcycle block).
- Goal: improve internal efficiency, motor pattern mastery, reduce perceived effort — NOT external change.
- IF final week of step feels significantly easier + athlete executes with higher RIR than week 1 → step complete; increase load for next block.
- Best for: heavy Tier 1 lifts (squat, DL, OHP), peaking phases, highly advanced lifters.

**Rules — Deload/Fatigue modification:**
- Auto-regulatory volume drop (low-readiness day): reduce total working sets ~30–50%.
- Scheduled deload: every 4–6 weeks (or earlier if performance plateaus); cut total volume 30–50%; reduce load 5–15%; maintain movement patterns (NO novel exercises during deload).
- IF older athlete or very high absolute strength → deload every 3–4 weeks.

**Rules — Frequency:**
- Train each pattern 1.5–2× per week (general optimal for advanced trainees).
- Two hard exposures to same pattern per week need ≥ 48–72 hrs between them with one session clearly lighter.

**Coach review triggers:**
- Primary Tier 1 lift stagnates reps or load ≥ 3 consecutive weeks despite adequate recovery
- Any barbell movement → shooting pain, numbness, tingling
- Continuous loss of neutral spinal alignment or major L/R asymmetry at loads > 75% 1RM

**Goal-agnostic note:** Tier structure applies to any goal (strength, hypertrophy, maintenance, return-to-sport). Tier 1 choice changes by goal class (e.g., OAP/HSPU for calisthenics athletes, big barbell lifts for strength athletes, weighted pull-up for weighted calisthenics). Examples are goal illustrations only.

---

## 5. RIR & Autoregulation

**PRINCIPLE:** RIR (Reps in Reserve) is the primary tool for matching external load to internal readiness; it protects the training stimulus without requiring fixed percentages. (Art. 5)

**Definitions (internal):**
- RIR: estimate of additional clean repetitions possible before TECHNICAL failure (not absolute muscular failure)
- Technical failure: position breakdown, bar path deviation, or significant deceleration — ends the set regardless of whether muscle could grind another ugly rep
- RPE/RIR inverse relationship: RPE 8 = RIR 2; RPE 7 = RIR 3; RPE 9 = RIR 1; RPE 10 = RIR 0

**Rules — RIR Scale:**

| RIR | Description | Bar Speed Signal | Programmatic Use |
|---|---|---|---|
| 0 | Zero reps left; maximal grind | Very slow or stalled | Planned test only; rare on heavy compounds; OK occasional isolation |
| 1 | One rep left; bar visibly slow on final rep | Clearly slow final rep | Peak of strength block, Tier 1 end-of-block; not weekly default |
| 2 | Two reps left; final rep requires focus but speed stable | Some deceleration | Standard default for primary barbell strength work |
| 3 | Three reps left; weight heavy but execution smooth/explosive | Minimal deceleration | New block intro, accessory work, high sport stress weeks |
| 4+ | Four+ reps left; maximal velocity | Fast, confident | Power sets, warm-ups, deload weeks, active recovery |

**Rules:**
- IF Tier 1 barbell compound → operate RIR 1–3 in most weeks; RIR 0 maximum once every 3–4 weeks as planned exposure.
- IF Tier 3 isolation (curl, triceps press-down, etc.) → RIR 0–1 on last set workable when joint tolerance acceptable and recovery permits.
- IF bar speed drops sharply mid-session before target RIR → drop load 5–10% on remaining sets to maintain RIR target.
- IF rep prescribed (e.g., 5 reps) but at target RIR → do NOT increase load until top of rep range achieved across ALL sets with correct RIR AND clean technique.
- IF on a day when sport fatigue is elevated → same RIR target will naturally select a lower absolute weight; preserve the relative stimulus.
- IF RIR load consistently drops for ≥ 3 consecutive weeks → accumulated fatigue too high; trigger deload or volume reduction.

**Autoregulation rules for BJJ/hybrid athletes:**
- Day after hard sparring → RIR 2 target will self-select lower absolute load; do NOT force previous week's numbers.
- Grip fatigue from grappling → use straps to ensure the target muscles (back, posterior chain) receive the intended stimulus.

**Goal-agnostic note:** RIR applies across all goal classes. For conditioning autoregulation, analogous tool is heart-rate zone or breathing/conversation-ability scale (RPE 4–5 of 10 = Zone 2). RIR is resistance-training-specific.

---

## 6. Double Progression & Step Loading

**PRINCIPLE:** Progression should advance reps before load (double progression) or hold load static to solidify neural efficiency before advancing (step loading). (Art. 6)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Double progression: volume-first, then intensity; rep range has lower and upper bounds
- Step loading: hold load AND reps constant for entire block (2–4 weeks); goal is reduced perceived effort, improved bar speed, better motor pattern — THEN advance load

**Rules — Double Progression cycle:**
1. Assign load + rep range (e.g., 3×4–6 at RIR 2).
2. Athlete works within range; tracks reps and RIR per set.
3. Build reps across sessions toward top of range.
4. IF top of range achieved for ALL sets with clean technique AND target RIR → increase load by 2–5%.
5. With new heavier load → reps return to bottom of range; rebuild upward.
- Rep ranges must be tight: 4–6, 6–8, 8–10, 10–12 — NOT wide ranges (e.g., 5–12).
- IF reps hit top but form broke down on final reps → do NOT progress load; hold.

**Rules — Step Loading cycle:**
1. Assign specific load + strict sets/reps (e.g., 4×4 at RIR 2).
2. Hold volume AND intensity STATIC for full block (typically 2–3 weeks).
3. Focus on bar speed, technique refinement, reducing perceived effort.
4. IF week 3 feels significantly more explosive + stable than week 1 (subjective RIR higher) → step complete; increase load for next block.
- Step loading jump: typically 5–10 lbs (concrete example from Art. 6: 225 lb front squat step, then +5–10 lbs).
- Step blocks: 2–4 weeks typical; not a fixed timer — base decision on performance feel.

**Load increase sizing:**
- Standard: 2–5% load increase (reliable default for most Tier 1 and Tier 2 lifts).
- Absolute: 5–10 lbs for barbell Tier 1; 2.5–5 lbs for dumbbell Tier 2; micro-plates if available.

**Which model to use:**

| Model | Best Use Case | Primary Lift Examples |
|---|---|---|
| Double Progression | Intermediate–advanced; Tier 2; hypertrophy phases | DB Floor Press, BSS, RDL, Split Squat |
| Step Loading | Highly advanced; Tier 1; peaking phases; lifts progressing slowly | Front Squat, Trap-Bar DL, OHP, Weighted Pull-Up |

**Coach review triggers:**
- Fails to add a single rep OR improve RIR across a full 4-week step block despite adequate recovery
- Continuous form degradation when pushing top of double progression range

**Goal-agnostic note:** Both models apply to any goal. For calisthenics athletes, double progression applies to skill progressions (e.g., archer pull-up building reps) and step loading applies to held skill holds (e.g., OAP lockoff holds at same positions across a block before extending ROM).

---

## 7. Deload & Fatigue Management

**PRINCIPLE:** Scheduled deloads are proactive recovery phases that dissipate accumulated neuromuscular fatigue and reveal strength adaptations built in the preceding block. (Art. 7)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Deload: deliberate, structured reduction in training stress; NOT passive rest, NOT random light days
- Functional overreaching: necessary state during accumulation blocks; CANNOT be sustained indefinitely
- Overreaching: exceeded when performance drops, readiness falls, and RIR targets are consistently missed

**Rules — When to Deload:**
- IF ≥ 4–6 weeks of progressive accumulation without deload → schedule deload (default timing for most advanced athletes).
- IF older athlete OR very high absolute loads OR heavy competition camp → deload every 3–4 weeks.
- IF performance plateaus → trigger deload; assess block structure.
- IF readiness remains low + RIR targets consistently missed + movement quality declines → trigger early deload.
- IF returning from deload and strength still dropping → requires longer recovery phase + nutrition/sleep review.

**Rules — What to Reduce:**
- Volume: cut total working sets 30–50% across all exercises (e.g., 4×5 → 2×5).
- Intensity: drop load 5–15% from heaviest previous week; OR increase RIR target (e.g., RIR 1 end of block → RIR 3–4 during deload).
- Novelty: NO new exercises during deload; new movements cause soreness and defeat recovery purpose.
- Movement patterns: maintain SAME exercises as previous block (motor pattern preservation).
- RIR feel during deload: bar should feel FAST, explosive, highly manageable.

**Rules — What NOT to Do:**
- Do NOT take 1RM tests during a deload.
- Do NOT deload weight room while simultaneously doubling sparring or sprint intervals.
- Do NOT wait until performance crashes to deload (proactive > reactive).
- Do NOT drop load too much (more than 15% often feels detrained; volume is the primary lever, not load).

**Numeric example (Art. 7):**
- Peak accumulation week: Trap-Bar DL 4×5 at RIR 1–2.
- Deload week: Trap-Bar DL 2×5 at RIR 3–4; load reduced ~10%.

**Readiness recovery benchmark:** Fatigue markers (resting HR, grip strength, subjective readiness) should return to baseline by END of deload week. If not → athlete may be in deeper overreaching.

**Goal-agnostic note:** Deload logic applies universally. For sport athletes, the weight-room deload often coincides with high sport stress weeks (competition camp, travel); reducing gym volume preserves recovery for the sport without abandoning training.

---

## 8. Strength vs. Hypertrophy Loading

**PRINCIPLE:** Strength and hypertrophy are distinct adaptations requiring different programming environments; advanced athletes benefit from periodically emphasizing one over the other. (Art. 8)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Strength loading: high intensity (relative load), low reps, long rest — primary adaptation = neural efficiency + motor unit recruitment
- Hypertrophy loading: moderate intensity, moderate-high reps, shorter rest — primary adaptation = muscle cross-sectional area increase via mechanical tension + sufficient volume
- Functional hypertrophy: mass gains that directly increase force production and structural durability without excess non-contractile weight

**Rules — Programming parameters:**

| Goal | Load (%1RM) | Rep Range | RIR | Rest Period | Best Tier |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Strength-biased | 75–90% | 3–6 reps | 1–3 | 3–5 min | Tier 1 compounds |
| Hypertrophy-biased | 60–80% | 6–15 reps | 0–3 | 90 sec–2 min | Tier 2 and Tier 3 |
| Mixed athletic block | Tier 1 = strength params; Tier 2/3 = hypertrophy params | — | — | — | All tiers |

**Rules:**
- IF Tier 1 barbell compound → default to strength-biased loading (3–6 reps; high intensity; long rest).
- IF Tier 2/Tier 3 accessory → may be hypertrophy-biased; pushed closer to failure when joint tolerance + recovery acceptable.
- IF strength block → prioritize technical quality and bar speed; avoid pushing toward technical failure.
- IF hypertrophy block → emphasize controlled eccentric phase to maximize mechanical tension.
- IF both qualities needed (mixed block) → strength-focused Tier 1 work ALWAYS first in the session (max neural output preserved).
- IF Tier 1 lifts technically clean but stalled → consider hypertrophy block to build prime mover size as next lever.
- IF athlete feels "big but inefficient under heavy loads" → shift to strength block with lower volume and heavier loading.
- IF sport athlete in-season / high mat volume → prioritize strength-biased loading (maximize force production within current bodyweight; minimize unnecessary soreness from high-volume hypertrophy work).
- IF hypertrophy needed for sport athletes → use low-eccentric, low-joint-stress accessory choices (sled work, machine rows) to reduce soreness carryover into sport sessions.

**Goal-agnostic note:** "Strength" and "hypertrophy" are universal categories. For weight-class sport athletes, prioritize strength (max force per unit mass). For structural durability purposes (tendons, off-season tissue building), hypertrophy phases serve all goals. For endurance athletes, hypertrophy choices should be low-eccentric-cost.

---

## 9. Training Max & 1RM Estimation

**PRINCIPLE:** A training max (TM) set conservatively below true 1RM is the anchor for sustainable intensity calculations; 1RM testing is reserved, not default. (Art. 9)

**Definitions (internal):**
- True 1RM: single-day peak force production snapshot; reflects peak state, not average readiness
- Training Max (TM): 90–95% of verified/estimated 1RM; used as calculation anchor; creates buffer for readiness fluctuation
- Estimated 1RM: predicted from submaximal performance; preferred over live 1RM testing for safety and fatigue management
- Mismatch effect: plan prescribes weight that is too heavy for athlete's current sport-induced readiness state

**Rules:**
- TM = 90–95% of verified/estimated 1RM (default starting range).
- IF using TM for Tier 1 load calculation → working sets land ~80–85% of true 1RM (high motor unit recruitment without excessive fatigue).
- IF athlete feels sluggish on a given day → apply Daily Autoregulation Rule: use load that satisfies target RIR with clean form, regardless of what TM calculation prescribes.
- IF bar speed noticeably slower than previous week at same load → TM is likely set TOO HIGH; adjust TM downward for next block.
- IF RIR targets consistently met too easily (hitting 5–6 reps when program calls for 3) → TM is likely set TOO LOW; update TM upward.
- IF athlete performing 3–5 reps at RIR 1–2 submaximal → use that set to estimate 1RM; do NOT need to test to true failure.
- Update TM every 4–8 weeks depending on performance trends (RIR accuracy + technical consistency).
- Test/re-estimate 1RM every 12–16 weeks for advanced athletes (NOT monthly).
- IF 3 consecutive training blocks show no estimated 1RM progress → coach review.

**1RM estimation method (Art. 9):**
- Use 3–5 reps at RIR 1–2 clean set → plug into rep-max formula → estimate 1RM.
- "Clean max" (technically proficient, full ROM, controlled) ≠ "gym max" (grinding, shaky, compromised position); TM should be based on clean max only.

**Goal-agnostic note:** TM logic applies to all barbell and weighted calisthenics work (e.g., weighted pull-up TM). For sport athletes managing weight cuts, a conservative TM explicitly prevents overloading on reduced-recovery days.

---

## 10. Autoregulation — Real-Time Load, Volume & RIR Adjustment

**PRINCIPLE:** Autoregulation is a disciplined feedback loop — the plan provides target stimulus; the session provides a real signal; adjustments protect the stimulus by changing controllable variables in response to actual readiness. (Art. 10)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Top set: heaviest planned working set in a session, anchored to an RIR target rather than fixed percentage
- Back-offs: subsequent sets at reduced load (typically top set − 8–12%) at slightly higher RIR
- Bar speed cue: primary objective stop signal — if bar speed drops sharply before target RIR is reached, the day is heavier than planned

**Rules — Session-Level Autoregulation:**
- Anchor main lifts to RIR targets (not fixed %). Example prescription: "top single at RIR 1 → 3 back-off sets at −8–10%, RIR 2."
- IF warm-up confirms plan (bar feels light, speed good) → take top set at UPPER end of workable load range.
- IF warm-up suggests heavier-than-planned day → take top set at LOWER end of workable range.
- Do NOT adjust mid-set; adjust BETWEEN sets.
- Exception for stopping mid-set: clear technical breakdown, sharp pain, sudden loss of control.
- IF first work set confirms planned RIR → continue planned volume.
- IF second back-off set misses RIR by > 1 → cut one set OR reduce load on remaining sets.

**Rules — Volume Autoregulation:**
- TRIM VOLUME before reducing load: cutting sets is a smaller deviation from the planned adaptation than cutting load.
- IF readiness is high → complete prescribed sets.
- IF sport stress elevated this week → preserve key Tier 1 exposures; Tier 2 and Tier 3 are first cuts.
- IF first work set misses RIR target → hold load on main lift; cut one back-off set; remove secondary main lift if present.
- IF technical execution breaks down before RIR target reached → STOP lift; do not grind; route to coach review before next session.

**Rules — Readiness Classification → Action:**

| Reported Readiness + Warm-Up Signal | Action |
|---|---|
| Clean recovery confirmed by warm-up | Run session as written |
| Moderate fatigue confirmed | Keep main lift; reduce 1–2 accessory sets |
| High fatigue OR first work set misses RIR | Hold main lift load; cut one back-off; remove secondary main lift |
| Sport stress elevated for week | Preserve Tier 1; reduce Tier 2 and Tier 3 before changing Tier 1 |
| Technical execution breakdown before RIR | Stop lift; route to coach review |

**Rules — Logging:**
- Log top-set load, RIR achieved, and bar speed observation after every main lift session.
- Without these data points → next session's anchor is a guess.

**Goal-agnostic note:** Autoregulation is especially critical for hybrid athletes whose readiness varies session-to-session due to multiple stress sources. Beginners may autoregulate accessory volume only; main lift prescriptions should be more fixed until RIR estimation is accurate.

---

## 11. BJJ Exercise Selection

**PRINCIPLE:** For BJJ athletes, select exercises that buy the most sport-relevant strength per unit of fatigue — tracking four concurrent costs: grip, shoulder load, lower-back load, total systemic stress. (Art. 11)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Four cost axes (BJJ-specific): grip fatigue, shoulder load, lower-back load, total systemic stress
- Transfer: degree to which the adaptation from a gym exercise improves sport performance

**Rules:**
- IF shoulder irritated from sparring → prefer neutral-grip and landmine pressing over flat barbell; DB and ring variants are reliable defaults; heavy flat barbell becomes a rotation, not a constant.
- IF grip fatigued from rolling → use straps on pulling work; substitute grip-heavy accessories for chest-supported or machine variants.
- IF lower-back stiffness → substitute heavy barbell hinges with hip thrusts, back extensions, or moderate-load trap-bar work; reduce direct lumbar volume.
- IF sport stress high → preserve Tier 1 selections; reduce Tier 2 and Tier 3 volume before changing Tier 1 variation.
- NEVER stack heavy deadlift + heavy barbell row + heavy grip-heavy carry in same session (overlap of same tissues).
- IF heavy bilateral barbell work + hard mat day within 24 hrs → combined fatigue cost is high even if loads look reasonable in isolation.
- Pulling selection: prefer chest-supported or strap-assisted over heavy double-overhand on weeks with hard sparring (move fatigue cost OUT of grip and INTO target back muscles).
- Lower-body selection should include split-stance and single-leg patterns (maps to passing, sweeping, stand-up).
- Rotate variations on a BLOCK basis (3–6 weeks), not session-by-session; without sufficient exposures, athlete cannot autoregulate, progress, or read true RIR signal.

**Practical selection defaults for BJJ:**
- Squat Tier 1: Front Squat, Safety-Bar Squat, or Box Squat (lower shoulder demand than high-bar back squat during sparring weeks)
- Hinge Tier 1: Trap-Bar DL (grip options), Block Pull, or Hip Thrust (when LB load high)
- Press Tier 1: Neutral-Grip DB Bench, Landmine Press (heavy flat barbell is a rotation for off-peak weeks)
- Pull Tier 1: Weighted Pull-Up with straps or neutral grip, Chest-Supported Row
- Unilateral: Reverse Lunge, Split Squat, Step-Up (lower-body); Single-Arm DB Row (upper-body)

**Goal-agnostic note:** The four-cost-axis model (grip, shoulder, LB, systemic) is specific to grappling/contact sport. For other goal classes, the relevant cost axes differ (e.g., for runners: knee/ankle impact, hip flexor fatigue, IT-band stress).

---

## 12. Training Frequency

**PRINCIPLE:** Optimal lifting frequency for sport athletes is the highest frequency that hits each pattern often enough to progress while allowing adequate recovery between hard exposures — not the maximum possible sessions. (Art. 12)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Session frequency: number of lifting sessions per week
- Pattern frequency: number of times each major pattern (squat, hinge, push, pull) is loaded per week
- Pattern exposure: any loaded session for that pattern, including lighter second-exposure sessions

**Rules — Frequency Selection:**
- IF sport athlete with high weekly sport volume → 2–3 lifting sessions/week is typically the optimal range.
- IF BJJ athlete with 5–6 mat sessions/week → 3 lifting sessions is a strong default for serious competitors.
- 4 lifting sessions/week = upper limit for most sport athletes; only viable when sport volume is low, real recovery between sessions exists, or 2 of 4 sessions are short/targeted.
- 2 well-run sessions/week consistently outperform 4 poorly recovered sessions for combat athletes.
- 4 lifting sessions/week for off-season or athletes with light competition schedules.
- Competition week → 1–2 short sessions (pattern exposure only; goal is maintenance, not progression).

**Rules — Pattern Coverage:**
- Each of squat, hinge, push, pull must be trained ≥ 1× per week (never miss a pattern for multiple consecutive weeks).
- Ideal: ≥ 2 exposures per pattern per week for advanced athletes needing progression in that quality.
- ADD a second pattern exposure to an existing session BEFORE adding a new lift day.
- Pattern repetition: pair heavy exposure with lighter/different-variation exposure; NOT two heavy same-pattern sessions close together.
- Two heavy same-pattern sessions: must be ≥ 48–72 hrs apart; one must be clearly lighter than the other.

**Rules — Sport Constraints:**
- IF mat sessions + runs already fill 5–6 days → lifting frequency should be 2–3, not 4.
- IF competition within 4 weeks → hold or REDUCE session count rather than adding sessions to "make up" missed work.
- Conditioning sessions compete for the SAME recovery budget as lifting; they must be factored into frequency decisions.

**Rules — Frequency as a Range:**
- 3 sessions/week during normal training → may drop to 2 during competition push; pattern coverage stays similar.
- Adding a 4th/5th lift day to a saturated sport week rarely returns more strength than it takes from recovery.

**Goal-agnostic note:** Frequency rules apply to all goal classes. For endurance-first athletes (runners, cyclists), lifting frequency often sits at 2 sessions/week to protect running volume. For purely strength-focused athletes without a sport, 4 sessions/week is more viable.

---

## 13. Volume Landmarks — MEV, MAV, MRV

**PRINCIPLE:** Weekly volume per major pattern for advanced lifters exists within a productive range with clear lower and upper bounds; exceeding the upper bound reduces quality faster than it drives adaptation. (Art. 13)

**Definitions (internal):**
- MEV (Minimum Effective Volume): smallest weekly set count still driving progress; lower bound of productive range
- MAV (Maximum Adaptive Volume): dose at which adaptation is maximized; somewhere within the productive range
- MRV (Maximum Recoverable Volume): largest set count the athlete can recover from before the next session; upper bound — exceeding this erodes quality
- Hard working set: taken at approximately RIR 1–3; close enough to failure to drive adaptation; excludes warm-ups, light technique sets, and sets at RIR ≥ 4

**Rules — Weekly Volume Ranges:**
- Advanced lifters with sport: 8–16 hard working sets per major pattern per week is the productive working range.
- LOWER end (8 sets/pattern/week): appropriate during heavy sport blocks.
- UPPER end (16 sets/pattern/week): appropriate during off-season or low-sport-stress phases.
- Tier 1 specifically: 4–8 hard working sets per week per Tier 1 pattern (more Tier 1 sets rarely the answer at advanced training age).
- Tier 2/3: remainder of weekly per-pattern total; allocated against weak points, structural balance, hypertrophy targets.
- Conditioning sessions count against the SAME weekly recovery budget; 30–40 min/week hard intervals = less available lifting volume.

**Rules — Volume Management:**
- CUT VOLUME before cutting load when readiness drops.
- START at lower end of working range; earn upper end by demonstrating the lower dose no longer drives progress with clean execution.
- Warm-up sets do NOT count toward weekly volume landmarks (sets at RIR ≥ 4 excluded).
- IF volume stalls progress → deload first, then test small increase next block; do NOT reactively add sets.
- Reassess volume every 3–6 weeks; if current dose stops producing progress → deload → small increase test.
- IF joint irritation appears alongside recent volume increase → return to previous dose for one block before retesting.
- IF competition within 4 weeks → taper accessory volume first, then secondary lift volume, while preserving Tier 1 exposure.

**Warning signals for MRV exceeded:**
- Strength on key lifts stagnates or drops
- Readiness falls
- Sleep disturbance
- Technical execution erodes
- Joint irritation appears or worsens

**Emergency reduction:** Reduce weekly working sets 30–50% for 1–2 weeks → route to coach/clinician if indicators persist.

**Goal-agnostic note:** Volume landmarks shift by goal. Off-season hypertrophy → upper range. In-season sport maintenance → lower range. High-intensity conditioning athletes need their conditioning volume counted into the budget. These ranges are starting points, not rigid prescriptions.

---

## 14. Intensity Zones

**PRINCIPLE:** Intensity in strength training = load relative to current capacity; it is best anchored to RIR + rep range rather than fixed percentages, and its upper boundary shifts downward during heavy sport blocks. (Art. 14)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Intensity: NOT the same as effort; a set of 5 at RIR 0 is HIGH intensity and HIGH effort; a set of 10 at RIR 0 is MODERATE intensity and HIGH effort
- Strength qualities → respond to higher loads with high intent, proximity to limit, without frequent failure
- Hypertrophy → responds across a wider load range as long as proximity to failure is sufficient
- Power-biased work: low reps (singles/doubles), HIGH RIR (≥ 3), maximal bar speed intent

**Rules — Intensity Zones:**

| Zone | Rep Range | RIR | Adaptation Target | Rest | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Maximal strength | 1–5 reps | 1–3 | Neural efficiency, force production | 3–5 min | Tier 1; RIR 0 planned rarely (once per 2–3 weeks as maximum) |
| Strength-hypertrophy | 3–6 reps | 1–3 | Motor unit + tissue | 2–4 min | Tier 1 transition zones |
| Hypertrophy | 6–12 reps | 1–3 | CSA, mechanical tension | 90 sec–2 min | Tier 2 and Tier 3 default |
| Endurance/tissue capacity | 12–20+ reps | 0–2 | Metabolic adaptation, tissue capacity | 60–90 sec | Tier 3; isolation |
| Power/speed | 1–3 reps | ≥ 3 | Rate of force development | Full (2–4 min) | Move with INTENT; not a strength session |

**Rules:**
- IF Tier 1 strength block → build to top set of 1–3 at RIR 1 → back-offs 3–5 reps at top set −8–12%, RIR 2.
- IF Tier 2 hypertrophy block → sets of 8–10 at RIR 2 is clean default.
- IF power-biased work → singles/doubles at RIR ≥ 3, moved with maximum intent; adding more sets/reps converts to low-quality strength session.
- IF sport stress high → cap intensity ceiling: RIR 0 work OFF the program; RIR 1–2 is practical upper bound during competition prep.
- IF bar speed clearly slow at warm-ups → drop planned top set by one RIR target; reduce back-off load.
- IF competition within 2–4 weeks → prefer power-biased intensity over strength-biased intensity for at least one weekly lift session.
- IF high-intensity work consistently disrupts recovery → reduce frequency of RIR 0–1 work before adjusting other variables.
- IF intensity anchored to old max calculated months ago → adjust; strength shifts faster than that.
- True RIR 0 set on main lift: maximum once every 2–3 weeks; never a weekly default.

**Goal-agnostic note:** Intensity zones shift by goal and athlete type. Endurance athletes use the lower-intensity zones for most barbell work. Powerlifters spend more time in zones 1–2. Sport athletes in-season shift toward power and maintenance zones. The anchor is RIR, not fixed percentage — this adapts the prescription to current readiness regardless of goal class.

---

## 15. Training to Failure

**PRINCIPLE:** Failure is a narrow-use tool; on advanced heavy compound lifts it adds recovery cost without proportional adaptation gain; it belongs sparingly on low-systemic-cost isolation work. (Art. 15)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Technical failure: set ends when technique fails (position breakdown = failure for programming purposes), NOT when muscle cannot produce another ugly rep
- Absolute failure: no further reps possible regardless of technique
- Recovery cost of failure: assessed not by the set itself but by quality of the next 24–72 hours of training

**Rules:**
- IF Tier 1 heavy compound (squat, DL, press) → keep at RIR ≥ 1 most weeks; RIR 0 is a planned exposure ONCE every 3–4 weeks (end-of-block check); NOT a weekly event.
- IF Tier 2 compound (split squat, RDL, chest-supported row) → failure on LAST set is workable but not necessary; RIR 1 captures most of the benefit at lower cost.
- IF Tier 3 isolation (curl, leg extension, lateral raise, hamstring curl) → failure on last set of last exercise is reasonable when: joint tolerance OK + sport stress low + off-season.
- IF sport stress high (heavy mat week, competition prep) → REMOVE failure work from entire program; set RIR floor at 2 across all lifts.
- IF failure work disrupts next session quality consistently → move failure earlier in week or remove from block.
- IF joint irritation appears on a lift used for failure work → remove failure from that lift; route to clinician if irritation persists.
- STOP set when technique fails — even if muscle could grind another rep. The positionally broken rep is NOT the quality marker for the set.
- Log the recovery cost: track readiness, bar speed in next session, sport performance after a failure exposure.

**Failure dosing rules:**
- Low-sport-stress / off-season: occasional failure on isolation at end of session = reasonable.
- In-season / heavy sport blocks: RIR floor of 2 across all lifts.
- Failure on Tier 1 = maximum once every 3–4 weeks as planned check.
- Failure on Tier 2 = occasional, last set, when warranted.
- Failure on Tier 3 = workable when isolation, low systemic cost, off-season.

**Goal-agnostic note:** Failure rules apply across goal classes. For calisthenics athletes chasing skill-based movements (OAP, planche), failure on primary skill holds is counterproductive (technical breakdown reinforces bad patterns). Failure on isolated assistance work (curls, triceps, deltoid) remains workable.

---

## 16. Periodization

**PRINCIPLE:** Block-based periodization organizes training into 3–6 week phases with a single clear emphasis (hypertrophy, strength, peak, maintain), sequenced backward from the competition date. (Art. 16)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Hypertrophy block: volume emphasis, moderate loads, RIR 1–3, Tier 1 sets 4–6 reps, Tier 2/3 higher rep
- Strength block: intensity emphasis, heavier loads, RIR 1–3, Tier 1 sets 1–5 reps, volume drops vs. hypertrophy block
- Peaking block: compressed volume, power expression emphasis, very short Tier 1 sets (singles/doubles), minimal accessory
- Maintain block: key patterns at moderate volume + intensity; used during competition or high-sport-stress phases; goal = preservation, not progression

**Rules — Block Structure:**
- Standard microcycle length: 3–4 weeks of training + 4–7 day deload.
- Three blocks of this type ≈ 12–16 week full cycle.
- Block sequence for competition prep: [Hypertrophy] → [Strength] → [Peak/Maintain] → [Competition].
- IF sport calendar unpredictable → use shorter blocks (3–4 weeks) + flexible peaking block compressible to 1 week if competition added late.

**Rules — Within Blocks:**
- Hold exercise selection and frequency STABLE within a block.
- The variable that changes BLOCK to BLOCK: usually intensity, volume, or proximity to failure (not all three simultaneously).
- End EACH block with a planned deload (4–7 days reduced volume + intensity).
- Tier 1 patterns (squat, hinge, push, pull) should be exposed in EVERY block, even if variation or dose changes.

**Rules — Responding to Disruption:**
- IF sport stress unexpectedly rises mid-block → convert remainder to maintain emphasis rather than abandoning block.
- IF readiness drops sharply mid-block → advance deload by 1 week.
- IF progress stalls in two consecutive blocks → extend next deload; reassess block sequence and total weekly load.
- IF competition added late → truncate current block; move directly into peaking; preserve Tier 1 exposures.

**Numeric examples (Art. 16):**
- 12-week active competitor cycle: Weeks 1–4 hypertrophy (RIR 2), Weeks 5–8 strength (RIR 1–2), Weeks 9–11 peaking (low volume, power, RIR 2–3 on heavy work), Week 12 competition.
- 8-week off-season BJJ cycle: Weeks 1–4 hypertrophy (6–10 reps, RIR 2), Weeks 5–7 strength (3–5 reps, RIR 1), Week 8 deload (volume halved, intensity moderate).

**Stacking rule:** Do NOT maximize intensity AND volume in the same block (recovery problem before adaptation lands).

**Goal-agnostic note:** Block sequence is goal-driven. For calisthenics/skill goals: skill-acquisition block → loaded-skill block → expression block. For endurance athletes: base aerobic block → threshold block → race-specificity block → taper. The logical structure — one emphasis per block, backward planning from goal date, deload between — is universal.

---

## 17. Peaking & Taper

**PRINCIPLE:** Peaking is a multi-week planned stress reduction to allow accumulated adaptations to surface; it is NOT a deload and must preserve intensity (not volume) to avoid arriving flat. (Art. 17)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Taper: planned, multi-week (2–3 weeks) reduction in training stress toward a competition date
- Deload vs. taper: deload = short recovery inside a block; taper = multi-week phase culminating in performance event
- Freshness target: the athlete should feel "slightly undertrained" on competition day — NOT overtrained or depleted

**Rules — Taper Structure:**

| Week Out | Volume | Intensity | RIR | Key Actions |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 3 weeks out (early taper) | Cut accessories 30–50% | Hold Tier 1 heavy | RIR 1–2 on main lifts | Shift conditioning toward sport-specific intervals |
| 2 weeks out | Cut Tier 1 volume another 30–50%; top sets → singles/doubles | Hold submaximal; power-biased | RIR 2–3 | Remove most accessory work |
| Competition week | Lightest week of cycle | Short, fast, submaximal power work | RIR ≥ 3 | 1–2 short sessions; final lift ≥ 48–72 hrs before competition |

**Rules:**
- IF readiness HIGH entering taper → run 2-week planned taper as written.
- IF readiness LOW entering taper → extend to 3 weeks; cut volume more aggressively in week 1.
- IF weight cut involved → cut accessory volume EARLIER; reduce conditioning intensity in final 7–10 days.
- IF competition added unexpectedly within 10 days → truncate to 1-week shortened taper emphasizing volume reduction + power expression.
- IF athlete reports excessive freshness with restlessness before competition → add one short, low-volume power session 48–72 hrs out.
- Replace near-failure work with power-biased work (singles/doubles at RIR ≥ 3) during taper.
- Hold Tier 1 pattern exposure to the LAST 7–10 days (removing a pattern in final week costs more than it saves).
- Cut ACCESSORIES before cutting MAIN LIFTS.
- Competition week = LIGHTEST week of entire cycle; this is NOT where to prove fitness.
- Hard BJJ sparring: stop 7–10 days out.
- For strength competition: last heavy pull ≤ 5–7 days out; heavy pulls inside that window rarely return more than they cost.
- Do NOT add new exercises during peak (competition not the place to discover how a new lift recovers).

**Goal-agnostic note:** Taper structure applies to any competitive goal: BJJ tournament, powerlifting meet, obstacle race, running event. Key logic (volume drops first, intensity preserved, power-biased final sessions, sport specificity holds longest) is universal across goal classes.

---

## 18. Accessory Programming

**PRINCIPLE:** Accessory work should be small, deliberate, and tied to a specific defined weakness; accessories are the first cut when sport stress rises. (Art. 18)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Tier 2 accessories: related patterns at lower systemic cost (e.g., split squat after back squat)
- Tier 3 accessories: targeted muscle or position work (e.g., Nordic curl, face pull, rotator cuff)
- Junk volume: accessory work that accumulates fatigue without driving a defined adaptation

**Rules:**
- Each accessory must solve a NAMED problem. If unable to name what it fixes → remove it from program.
- IF unable to answer "what specific weakness does this accessory address?" → it is junk volume.
- Cap: 3–5 accessory lifts per session; 2–4 working sets each at RIR 1–2 (ceiling, not requirement).
- Session length: 60–90 min for advanced athletes; > 90 min usually = too many accessories.
- Sequence: heavier/more systemic accessories FIRST; isolation/pump-style work LAST.
- DO NOT stack 3+ heavy bilateral accessories in a row in one session.
- Match accessory volume to block: strength block → accessory volume drops; hypertrophy block → accessory volume rises; peaking block → accessories shrink toward zero.
- Rotate accessories on BLOCK basis (3–6 weeks), NOT weekly (need enough exposures to progress and evaluate).
- When readiness drops → FIRST CUT is the last (least essential) accessory; Tier 1 holds.
- IF competition within 4 weeks → reduce accessory count and volume week by week.
- Avoid stacking grip-heavy accessories (farmer carries, heavy rows, dead hangs) in same session especially during hard sparring weeks.

**Standard session template:**
- 1 Tier 1 lift
- 1 Tier 2 lift (same pattern family)
- 1–2 Tier 3 lifts (weak points or structural balance)
- Optional isolation for hypertrophy (off-season only)

**BJJ-specific accessory selection priority:**
- Posterior chain at extended positions (RDL, single-leg RDL, back extension)
- Hip flexor under load (split squat, step-up, lunge)
- Trunk anti-rotation (Pallof press, suitcase carry)
- Grip endurance: scheduled AWAY from sparring days

**Goal-agnostic note:** Accessory selection is goal-specific. For powerlifters: close-grip bench, pause squat, deficit pull. For runners: hip strengthening, calf work, unilateral lower body. For BJJ: posterior chain, anti-rotation, grip. The decision framework (name the problem, match the pattern, right volume, right sequence) is universal.

---

## 19. Unilateral Training

**PRINCIPLE:** Unilateral work covers pattern gaps that bilateral barbell work leaves underdeveloped, manages side-to-side asymmetry, and builds positional stability for sport-specific demands. (Art. 19)

**Definitions (internal):**
- True single-leg: single-leg RDL, pistol squat — balance + stability + pattern integration
- Split-stance: split squat, reverse lunge, step-up — bilateral-style strength at lower systemic cost; maps to sport positions
- Single-arm pressing/pulling: independent loading + asymmetry diagnosis

**Rules:**
- Include 1–2 unilateral exposures per week for most sport athletes (lower-body + upper-body).
- Sequence: BILATERAL lifts FIRST; unilateral AFTER (reversing reduces quality of bilateral lift).
- Load by SUBORDINATE (weaker) side: prescribe load the weaker side can execute with clean position; dominant side does not drive the load.
- Prescribe "X reps PER SIDE" not total reps; keeps per-side quality focus and exposes asymmetry.
- IF split squat loses balance, forward lean, or shifts to dominant side → MORE informative than the load on the bar; the position is the data point.
- IF competition within 4 weeks → keep unilateral exposures; reduce volume; prefer split-stance over true single-leg (more stable under fatigue).
- IF recurrent joint irritation with specific unilateral pattern → substitute different unilateral pattern loading similar tissues.
- Log loads PER SIDE every session; a persistent widening gap is information for next block.
- A persistent, non-narrowing strength asymmetry = warrants review; a small gap that does not affect position or pattern = usually functional.
- DO NOT use unilateral work only when athlete is injured; by that point asymmetry has been compounding for months.

**Working dose:**
- 2–4 sets per side on 1–2 unilateral lifts per session.
- RIR 1–2 with clean execution (same standard as bilateral).
- Never loads unilateral lifts by what DOMINANT side can handle.

**Goal-agnostic note:** Unilateral work serves all athletes. For runners: single-leg RDL, step-up to address running-induced asymmetry. For powerlifters: BSS, reverse lunge as Tier 2 to shore up bilateral weak points. For BJJ: split-stance maps directly to passing/sweeping/stand-up. For calisthenics: archer variations, single-arm pressing/pulling as skill progressions.

---

## 20. Posterior Chain Development

**PRINCIPLE:** Posterior chain development requires rotating hinge variants, including hamstring work in BOTH lengthened AND shortened positions, and including glute-specific work — not just heavy deadlifts once a week. (Art. 20)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Posterior chain tissues: hamstrings, glutes, spinal erectors, supporting trunk musculature
- Lengthened position hamstring work (hip-dominant): RDL, single-leg RDL, good mornings — loads hamstring at hip
- Shortened position hamstring work (knee-dominant): Nordic curl, hamstring curl, sliding leg curl — loads hamstring at knee
- Glute-specific work: hip thrusts, glute bridges, banded variants — hip extension volume WITHOUT lower-back cost

**Rules:**
- Hinge pattern MINIMUM 1× per week in every weekly plan.
- Do NOT stack two heavy bilateral hinges per week for advanced sport athletes (rarely recovers cleanly).
- Pair: one heavy hinge + one moderate-load posterior chain accessory per week.
- IF sport stress high → substitute heavy barbell pull with hip thrust or moderate trap-bar work; reduce direct lower-back volume.
- IF lower-back stiffness present → prefer hip thrust + unilateral hinge over conventional pulls until stiffness clears.
- IF running volume high → add single-leg RDL work; limit heavy bilateral pulls to 1× per 10–14 days.
- Hip thrusts and barbell glute bridges: add hip extension volume WITHOUT lower-back or grip cost of conventional pulls — high value for grapplers.
- Heavy direct lumbar load (conventional DL, heavy good mornings) limits: do NOT run multiple heavy lumbar-loading sessions per week for active sport athletes.
- Back extensions at moderate load + reverse hyperextensions: useful low-cost lower-back additions.

**Weekly posterior chain template:**
- Tier 1: 1 heavy hinge variant (trap-bar DL, conventional DL, block pull)
- Tier 2: 1 moderate-load hip extension (hip thrust, B-stance RDL, single-leg RDL)
- Tier 3: 1 hamstring knee-flexion exercise (Nordic curl, hamstring curl)
- Optional: light back extension or reverse hyper

**Specific variants and their profiles:**
- Conventional DL: full posterior chain, HIGH systemic + grip cost
- Trap-bar DL: variable LB cost depending on handle height, setup, torso angle, individual mechanics (high-handle often changes cost profile more than low-handle); still high systemic cost
- RDL: hamstring in lengthened position, MODERATE systemic cost vs. floor pull
- Hip Thrust: glute-dominant, VERY LOW lower-back cost
- Nordic Curl: knee-flexion hamstring, HIGH hamstring stress (eccentric), use cautiously with hamstring strain history
- B-Stance RDL: unilateral bias with bilateral setup, moderate LB and grip cost

**Coach/clinician review triggers:**
- Sharp pain, radiating symptoms down leg during hinge work
- Persistent LB stiffness despite reduced volume
- Locking/giving-way in LB, hip, or knee during hinge or hip extension
- Major posterior chain asymmetry that worsens across a block

**Goal-agnostic note:** Posterior chain emphasis is relevant for all goal classes. BJJ uses it for posture/bridging/driving. Runners need it for stride economy and single-leg stability. Powerlifters need it for DL and squat performance. Calisthenics athletes need it for ring support, back levers, and planche balance.

---

## 21. Grip Strength Programming

**PRINCIPLE:** Grip strength for grapplers must be built WITHOUT saturating the small forearm tissues that mat work already stresses; scheduling relative to sparring is the primary variable. (Art. 21)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Crushing grip: closing the hand around an object
- Pinch grip: thumb opposing fingers (highest transfer for gi gripping)
- Supporting grip: holding a load over time (farmer carry equivalent)
- Wrist strength: pronation, supination, flexion, extension — contributes to load transfer and forearm irritation prevention

**Rules:**
- Total weekly grip load = mat work + lifting work + dedicated grip work; all three must fit within recovery budget.
- Place dedicated grip work on day FARTHEST from the next hard sparring session; minimum 48 hrs.
- Limit dedicated grip work to 2–3 sessions per week maximum.
- IF sport stress high → use STRAPS on all heavy pulls; REMOVE dedicated grip work for the week; mat work already provides sufficient grip stimulus.
- IF grip is the limiter on pulling lifts → add 1 targeted grip session/week; use straps on heavy pulling work.
- IF forearm/elbow stiffness or discomfort does NOT resolve within 48 hrs of grip work → reduce dose.
- IF competition within 4 weeks → reduce dedicated grip work; rely on mat exposure for grip stimulus.
- IF sparring volume unusually LOW for a stretch → add one extra grip session to capitalize on recovery window.
- DO NOT stack grip-heavy lifts (farmer carry, heavy barbell row, dead hang, double-overhand DL) in same session.

**Grip quality training tools:**
- Farmer carry (supporting grip): 3 sets × 30–40 sec, moderate load
- Gi pull-up (pinch + crushing grip): 3–5 sets × 3–5 reps; high recovery cost; schedule when grip has time before next mat work
- Towel pull/towel row (grip + pull pattern): high transfer for BJJ
- Pinch carry with weight plates: 3 sets × 20–30 sec
- Wrist curls + reverse wrist curls: forearm accessory

**Use-straps-vs-not decision:**
- Use straps when GOAL of lift = back/posterior chain development (grip is NOT the target)
- Use double-overhand or hook grip when GRIP IS ONE OF THE TARGETS for that session
- Fat-grip implements: useful in small doses; HIGH recovery cost on small tissues; off-season primarily

**Goal-agnostic note:** Grip programming is most specialized for grappling sports. For other goal classes (powerlifting, calisthenics), grip training decisions follow similar logic: target grip when it's the weakness, protect it when other structures are the training target, schedule grip-intensive work away from sessions requiring maximum grip performance.

---

## 22. Core/Anti-Rotation Training

**PRINCIPLE:** Core training for athletes should emphasize anti-rotation, anti-extension, anti-lateral flexion, and bracing under load — not dominantly direct flexion. (Art. 22)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Anti-rotation: resists trunk rotation while limbs move (Pallof press, suitcase carry, chop/lift)
- Anti-extension: resists lumbar hyperextension (hollow body, ab wheel rollout, dead bug)
- Anti-lateral flexion: resists lateral trunk bending (side plank, single-arm carry, single-arm overhead carry)
- Bracing under load: compound lifts trained with intentional IAP bracing (squat, DL, OHP)

**Rules:**
- Heavy compound lifts (squat, DL, OHP) trained with intentional bracing ARE core work; dedicated trunk work SUPPLEMENTS this base.
- Dedicated trunk work: cover all 4 anti-movement patterns (anti-rotation, anti-extension, anti-lateral flexion, bracing-under-load) across the week.
- Dose: 2–3 trunk exposures per week; 2–4 working sets each.
- Keep dedicated trunk sessions SHORT: 15–20 minutes if standalone; appendixed to lifting sessions when possible.
- Use TEMPO and POSITION over heavy external load for trunk work.
- Cap DIRECT FLEXION (sit-ups, weighted crunches): low priority for most sport athletes; grapplers already accumulate mat-related flexion volume; do NOT make direct flexion the dominant trunk modality.
- IF sport stress high → keep 1 short trunk session/week; emphasize anti-rotation + bracing; remove heavy direct flexion.
- IF lower-back stiffness → prefer anti-extension and anti-lateral flexion work; reduce loaded flexion.
- IF competition within 4 weeks → maintain trunk exposure (2 short sessions/week); remove novel exercises.
- IF asymmetry in trunk strength → start each set on subordinate side; adjust load to clean position on that side.
- Do NOT use only static positions (planks, hollow holds) — include dynamic anti-movement work.
- Breathing and bracing quality matter more than external load for trunk training effectiveness.

**Exercise-to-quality mapping:**

| Anti-Movement Quality | Key Exercises |
|---|---|
| Anti-rotation | Pallof press (half-kneeling, standing, split-stance), suitcase carry, cable chop/lift |
| Anti-extension | Hollow body hold, ab wheel rollout, dead bug variants |
| Anti-lateral flexion | Side plank variants, single-arm carry, single-arm overhead carry |
| Bracing under load | Loaded carries, paused squat, overhead carry, any compound with intentional bracing |
| Direct flexion (minor role) | Weighted sit-ups, cable crunches (small portion only) |

**Best single combination for BJJ/grappling:** Pallof press + ab wheel rollout + suitcase carry covers most high-transfer trunk qualities.

**Goal-agnostic note:** Anti-movement trunk training applies across all goal classes. For powerlifters, bracing under load and anti-rotation are directly relevant to maximal lift technique. For runners, anti-lateral flexion work addresses hip drop and single-leg stability. For calisthenics, hollow body and anti-extension work are direct prerequisites for planche, L-sit, and press progressions.

---

## 23. Conditioning Modalities

**PRINCIPLE:** Conditioning modalities are selected by transfer, recovery cost, and current lifting demands — not by default habit; most hybrid athletes benefit from 2–3 modalities per week. (Art. 23)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Modality selection: decision about WHICH conditioning tool to use (running, cycling, rowing, sled, circuit)
- Intensity selection: a SEPARATE decision from modality (any modality can be Zone 2 or intervals)
- Eccentric cost of modality: impact/muscle-damage component that competes with lifting recovery

**Rules — Modality Selection:**
- IF athlete is a runner by sport → running has highest specificity; also highest impact and interference cost.
- IF BJJ/grappling athlete (not a runner) → running is one option, NOT the only one; cycling, rowing, sled = adequate conditioning stimulus at lower joint cost.
- Pair heavy lower-body lifting days with LOW-IMPACT conditioning (cycling, rowing, incline walk).
- Pair lighter lifting days with HIGHER-IMPACT or higher-intensity conditioning (intervals, running).
- IF joint irritation appears with running → reduce running volume; substitute low-impact aerobic work.
- Sled work: UNUSUAL — offers significant conditioning effect WITHOUT eccentric load; highly compatible with heavy lifting blocks; one of highest-leverage tools for grappling athletes.
- Circuits: use SPARINGLY and deliberately; keep loads MODERATE; avoid heavy compound lifts within circuit format (compromises both strength and conditioning stimulus).
- Do NOT use only one conditioning modality (most hybrid athletes benefit from 2–3 different modalities per week).
- IF lifting volume rises significantly → REDUCE conditioning volume rather than maintaining both at full dose.

**Modality profiles:**

| Modality | Eccentric Cost | Interference Risk | BJJ Transfer | Best Use |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Running | HIGH | HIGH | Moderate | Runners; general conditioning; high-impact cost |
| Cycling | LOW | LOW | Moderate | Zone 2 base; compatible with heavy lifting |
| Rowing | MODERATE (back/grip) | MODERATE | HIGH | Intervals; aerobic conditioning; trunk endurance |
| Swimming | VERY LOW | VERY LOW | Moderate (breath) | Active recovery; aerobic base; breath control |
| Sled pushes/drags | VERY LOW | VERY LOW | HIGH | Repeat effort; leg drive; posterior chain; EC-free |
| Incline Walk | LOW | LOW | LOW | Zone 2 base; joint-friendly aerobic work |
| Circuits | MODERATE-HIGH | HIGH (if poorly designed) | HIGH (if positional) | Sport-specific work capacity |

**Goal-agnostic note:** For runners: running is non-negotiable; cycling and rowing supplement it for aerobic volume without added impact. For purely strength-focused athletes: conditioning is supplemental; sled and bike are the most compatible tools. For endurance athletes: all modalities have their place; the selection guides recovery from key workouts.

---

## 24. Zone 2 Conditioning

**PRINCIPLE:** Zone 2 is a specific physiological target (below first ventilatory/lactate threshold) that builds aerobic enzyme capacity, mitochondrial density, and inter-effort recovery — it is not "slow because it's easy." (Art. 24)

**Definitions (internal):**
- Zone 2 anchor criteria: conversation possible but slightly labored; nose breathing sustainable; RPE 4–5 out of 10; HR approximately 65–75% of max HR (rough starting range; breathing and conversation ability should guide final target more than fixed %)
- Zone 2 ≠ "junk cardio": specific physiological target with distinct adaptations from higher-intensity work
- First ventilatory/lactate threshold: the intensity above which this zone is exceeded

**Rules:**
- Zone 2 only produces Zone 2 adaptations IF athlete STAYS at target intensity; drifting up into Zone 3+ changes the stimulus AND the recovery cost.
- Use LOW-IMPACT modalities for Zone 2 most of the time (cycling, rowing, incline walk, elliptical); integrate cleanly with heavy lifting blocks.
- Build volume GRADUALLY: start 20–30 min/session; add 5–10 min/session over several weeks.
- Place Zone 2 AFTER hard sessions (lifting or sport) when possible, not immediately BEFORE (protect glycogen + readiness for higher-priority hard session).
- Separate Zone 2 from high-intensity sessions when same-day; stacking too close compromises high-intensity quality.

**Dosing guidelines:**
- Strength athletes (no sport): 2–3 sessions/week × 30–60 min (strong baseline; builds adaptation without competing with strength gains).
- Grapplers: 2–4 sessions/week × 30–45 min (base layer beneath sport-specific work); mornings after hard sparring are often ideal windows.
- Hybrid athletes (race-focused): Zone 2 is the largest single volume contributor to aerobic development across the year; dose individually.

**Practical anchors for Zone 2:**
- Heart rate: ~65–75% of max HR (rough range; breathing takes priority as anchor)
- Breathing: nose-breathing sustainable; conversational but slightly labored
- RPE: 4–5 out of 10
- Pacing: repeatable; should FINISH session feeling refreshed, NOT tired

**Rules during high sport stress:**
- IF sport stress high → keep Zone 2 in program; cap individual sessions at 30–40 min.
- IF readiness consistently high → add 5–10 min/session for 2 weeks; evaluate before further increases.
- IF joint irritation with a specific modality → rotate to lower-impact alternative (bike or row instead of running/walking).
- IF competition within 4 weeks → reduce Zone 2 volume; emphasize sport-specific quality.
- IF sleep/recovery markers IMPROVE with added Zone 2 → hold the new volume as baseline rather than reducing it.

**Daily Zone 2 feasibility:**
- Daily Zone 2 at 20–40 min can work for well-conditioned athletes using low-impact modalities (bike, incline walk, easy row).
- Daily running at Zone 2 = different stress profile; requires more caution and gradual build.

**Goal-agnostic note:** Zone 2 is one of the most underutilized tools across all strength-focused goal classes. Applies to powerlifters (between-set recovery, block-to-block recovery), calisthenics athletes (aerobic base shortens recovery time between skill sets), and all hybrid athletes.

---

## 25. High-Intensity Conditioning — Intervals, Repeat Power & Fatigue Management

**PRINCIPLE:** High-intensity conditioning has a narrow productive window for athletes managing strength and sport; it must be dosed deliberately, placed away from heavy lower-body sessions, and counted alongside sparring as part of total high-intensity budget. (Art. 25)

**Definitions (internal):**
- High-intensity conditioning: work intervals above second ventilatory threshold; athlete cannot sustain conversation; HR in upper aerobic and anaerobic zones
- Short intervals: 10–60 sec work, full or near-full recovery → highest transfer to grappling repeat-effort demand
- Classical intervals: 1–5 min work, work-to-rest ratio 1:1 to 1:3
- Repeat-effort work: multiple short bursts with managed recovery
- High-intensity continuous: 3–10 min at threshold

**Rules:**
- Cap: 1–2 gym-based high-intensity sessions per week for advanced athletes managing sport + strength; more usually costs more than it returns.
- Hard sparring COUNTS as high-intensity conditioning; total weekly high-intensity exposure = sparring rounds + gym intervals + competition simulation.
- IF active grappler with ≥ 3–4 hard sparring sessions/week → gym intervals may already be AT ceiling; additional gym-based HIIT is likely overcooking the dose.
- Place high-intensity conditioning ≥ 36–48 hrs from heavy lower-body lifting; ideally separate days.
- Minimum recovery between hard interval sessions: 48 hrs.
- Minimum recovery from hard session to competition simulation: 72 hrs.
- Sled-based intervals: highest lifting-compatibility for strength athletes (very low eccentric cost); tolerate stacking with lifting better than running or jumping intervals.
- IF high-intensity session the day before a heavy squat or pull → heavy lift pays the cost.
- Running intervals = highest eccentric cost; prefer bike, row, sled, ski erg for strength athletes who are not primarily runners.

**Output monitoring during sessions:**
- IF average power/speed/round quality drops ≥ 15–20% on final rounds → session is complete; do NOT continue.
- High-intensity sessions should be hard but REPEATABLE across rounds; NOT maximum-effort every round.
- Session length: most useful sessions = 15–30 min total including warm-up; work portion = 8–20 min accumulated hard work; if longer → intensity was not high enough.

**Interval templates (Art. 25 examples):**
- Off-season grappler: assault bike 10 × 30 sec hard / 90 sec easy (30 sec work : 90 sec rest = 1:3 ratio); stop if output drops > 15–20%.
- In-season strength-biased hybrid: sled push 6 × 20–30 yards, walk-back recovery; total session < 20 min.

**Adaptive rules:**
- IF sport sparring volume high this week → drop gym-based high-intensity to 1 short session OR remove entirely.
- IF athlete completed hard sport session within 24 hrs → gym high-intensity usually NOT appropriate that day.
- IF competition within 14 days → taper high-intensity volume; preserve only short, sharp exposures.
- IF recovery markers (sleep, resting HR, perceived energy) trend wrong direction across 2 weeks → cut high-intensity volume by HALF before adjusting anything else.

**Coach/clinician review triggers:**
- Persistent chest pain, lightheadedness, or unusual shortness of breath during/after intervals
- Heart rate fails to recover to expected range within several minutes of cessation across multiple sessions
- Joint irritation (knee, hip, ankle, foot) becomes localized and consistent with recent modality change
- Sleep, mood, or appetite degrade across a high-intensity block

**Goal-agnostic note:** High-intensity conditioning serves all goal classes needing work capacity. The dosing and modality logic is goal-specific (runners need running intervals; grapplers need repeat-short-effort work; triathletes need sport-specific intervals). The universal principles: cap at 1–2 sessions/week when also strength training, count sport stress toward total budget, prefer low-eccentric modalities when lifting is the primary goal.

---

## Supplemental: OAP / Elite Calisthenics Foundations (Art. 26 Preview)

*Note: Article 26 starts at line 4604, which is technically beyond the 25-article boundary. The below is captured from the lines ~4604 portion included in the reading and represents the beginning of the Cluster A content — included here as a bridge section for completeness where relevant decision rules appear.*

**PRINCIPLE:** The One-Arm Pull-Up (OAP) is a tendon project as much as a strength project; prerequisite gates must be cleared before specialization to avoid medial elbow irritation. (Art. 26)

**Prerequisite gates (all required before OAP specialization):**
- Strict 2-arm pull-ups: 3–5 reps at +50% bodyweight added (clean scapular control, no rib flare)
- Dead hang from 1 arm: 20–30 sec with controlled scapular depression, no shoulder hike
- 1-arm scapular pull (active hang to scapular set, no elbow bend): 8–10 controlled reps/side
- 90° 1-arm lockoff hold: 5–8 sec/side with controlled descent
- No active elbow flexor pain limiting load or ROM in past 6–8 weeks; if present → pause OAP-specific loading and pursue coach/clinician review

**OAP Programming parameters (starting ranges):**
- Frequency: 2 sessions/week per arm during specialization; 1/week during maintenance
- Working sets per arm per session: 3–5 quality sets; never grind to technical breakdown
- Reps per set: 1–3 for OAP and assisted OAP; 3–6 for archers and typewriters
- RIR: maintain RIR 1–2 on quality work; singles can be near-maximal but must look clean
- Eccentrics: 3–6 sec controlled descent; 1–3 reps per arm; 2–4 sets; ≥ 48–72 hrs between heavy sessions per arm
- Timeframe for athletes WITH prerequisites met: first OAP potentially achievable in ~2–4 months of focused specialization
- Timeframe for athletes WITHOUT prerequisite base: 12–24+ month project

*End of v10.9 Part 1 — Articles 1–25*

## PART B — Calisthenics Skills, Powerlifting Specialisation & Conditioning (Articles 26–52)

## One-Arm Pull-Up (OAP) Programming

**PRINCIPLE:** OAP is a tendon project as much as a strength project; multi-year timeline for most, 2–4 months only for already-qualified heavy pullers with clean elbow history. (Art. 26)

**Definitions (internal):** OAP = strict dead-hang concentric, chin above hand, no kip, no contralateral bar contact; asymmetry cap = ≤1 progression step gap between arms.

**Prerequisite gates (all must clear before specialization):**
- Weighted pull-up: +50 % BW × 3–5 strict reps
- One-arm dead hang: 20–30 sec, scapula depressed, no shoulder hike
- One-arm scapular pull (active hang → scapular set, no elbow bend): 8–10 reps/side
- 90° one-arm lockoff hold: 5–8 sec/side
- No elbow flexor pain limiting load/ROM in past 6–8 weeks → IF present, PAUSE OAP loading, route to clinician review

**Phase structure:**
| Phase | Duration | Key stimulus |
|---|---|---|
| 1 — Unilateral foundations | 8–12 wk | Hangs, scapular pulls, lockoff isometrics; archer pull-ups |
| 2 — Assisted OAP + eccentrics | 8–16 wk | Band/pulley-assisted OAP; 3–6 sec eccentrics, 1–3 reps/arm, 2–4 sets; 48–72 h between heavy sessions/arm |
| 3 — Half-range to full-range | 8+ wk | Half-range concentric from lockoff; multi-angle isometrics (90°, 120°, near-extended) |
| 4 — First OAP → consolidation | Open | Build 3 strict singles/arm before chasing reps |
| 5 — Maintenance / weighted OAP | Optional | Elite; continue tendon monitoring |

**Skill progression ladder:** Two-arm pull-up → weighted pull-up → archer pull-up → typewriter → towel-assisted OAP → band/pulley-assisted OAP → one-arm negative → multi-angle lockoffs → half-range OAP → full OAP.

**Rules:**
- IF adding OAP to program → REMOVE equivalent heavy bilateral pulling volume; total hard pulling volume must not increase.
- IF one arm is >1 progression step ahead → hold strong arm at maintenance until weaker arm catches.
- IF microloading → steps ≤5 % BW (or smaller).
- IF asymmetry in scapular control under load that was previously clean → PAUSE, clinician review.
- IF medial elbow soreness >7–10 days → PAUSE OAP-specific loading, clinician review.
- IF sharp anterior shoulder pain on hang initiation → PAUSE, review.
- IF numbness/tingling/grip loss in working arm → PAUSE, clinician review.

**Practical programming numbers:**
- Frequency: 2 sessions/week/arm (specialization); 1/week (maintenance)
- Sets/arm/session: 3–5 quality sets
- Reps/set: 1–3 for OAP/assisted OAP; 3–6 for archers/typewriters
- RIR: 1–2 on quality work; singles near maximal but technically clean
- Eccentric tempo: 3–6 sec (slower only as deliberate tendon stimulus)
- Isometric holds: 5–10 sec/position, 2–4 sets/arm
- Recovery: ≥48–72 h between heavy unilateral pulling sessions/arm; extend if elbow flexors cranky
- Eccentric cap: one 6-sec eccentric/arm is potent; stacking 6–10 often produces medial elbow irritation

**Template A (specialization, 2×/wk, clean elbow):**
- Day 1: Lockoff isometrics 3×6 sec at 90°, 120°, near-extended → Assisted OAP 4×2/arm at RIR 1–2 → Archer pull-up 3×5/arm
- Day 2: One-arm eccentric 3×1/arm, 6 sec lower → Weighted strict pull-up 4×3 at RIR 2 → Scapular pull 3×8

**Template B (maintenance, BJJ camp):**
- 1 session/wk: Archer 3×4/arm → Assisted OAP 3×1–2/arm at RIR 2 → Single-arm hang 2×20–30 sec/arm; skip heavy eccentrics

**Goal-agnostic note:** OAP specialization is one illustration of unilateral strength development. Same phase logic and tendon principles apply to any unilateral pulling or isometric capacity goal. Examples here are illustrations only.

---

## Weighted Pull-Up and Dips — Heavy Strength Progression

**PRINCIPLE:** Treat weighted pull-ups and dips as the upper-body anchor lifts of calisthenics (analogous to squat/deadlift/bench); microload, step-load, autoregulate by RIR, and cycle deloads. (Art. 27)

**Definitions (internal):** Anchor lift = session's highest-priority exercise, performed first; step loading = hold a load 2–3 weeks before adding; microloading = 0.5–2.5 kg jumps.

**Prerequisite gates:**
- Pull-up: 10 strict, full hang reps; dip: 10 controlled reps to tolerable depth, locked elbows at top
- Clean scapular pulls + prone Y/T/W 8–12 reps
- No active elbow/shoulder/wrist symptoms → IF present, PAUSE, clinician review

**Phase structure:**
| Phase | Duration | Rep range | Purpose |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 — Volume consolidation | 4–8 wk | 12–15 BW reps | Technique; connective tissue prep |
| 2 — Light weighted, hypertrophy | 8–12 wk | 6–10 reps | CT exposure, strength base |
| 3 — Strength block | 8–12 wk | 3–6 reps | Microload weekly or every other week |
